class="mediawiki ltr sitedir-ltr mw-hide-empty-elt ns-0 ns-subject mw-editable page-Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body rootpage-Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body skin-vector action-view"> <div class="noprint" id="mw-page-base"></div>\n<div class="noprint" id="mw-head-base"></div>\n<div class="mw-body" id="content" role="main">\n<a id="top"></a>\n<div class="mw-body-content" id="siteNotice"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Effect of spaceflight on the human body</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Weightless_hair.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="422" decoding="async" height="334" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Weightless_hair.jpg/220px-Weightless_hair.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Weightless_hair.jpg/330px-Weightless_hair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/Weightless_hair.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Weightless_hair.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Astronaut <a href="/wiki/Marsha_Ivins" title="Marsha Ivins">Marsha Ivins</a> demonstrates the effects of zero-G on her hair in space</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">Venturing into the environment of space</a> can have negative effects on the human body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20140127_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140127-1">[1]</a></sup> Significant adverse effects of long-term <a href="/wiki/Weightlessness" title="Weightlessness">weightlessness</a> include <a href="/wiki/Muscle_atrophy" title="Muscle atrophy">muscle atrophy</a> and deterioration of the <a href="/wiki/Human_skeleton" title="Human skeleton">skeleton</a> (<a href="/wiki/Spaceflight_osteopenia" title="Spaceflight osteopenia">spaceflight osteopenia</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spp22_15_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-spp22_15-2">[2]</a></sup> Other significant effects include a slowing of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_system" title="Cardiovascular system">cardiovascular system</a> functions, decreased production of <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Space_adaptation_syndrome" title="Space adaptation syndrome">balance disorders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Visual_impairment_due_to_intracranial_pressure" title="Visual impairment due to intracranial pressure">eyesight disorders</a> and changes in the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20190215_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20190215-3">[3]</a></sup> Additional symptoms include <a href="/wiki/Orthopnea" title="Orthopnea">fluid redistribution</a> (causing the "moon-face" appearance typical in pictures of astronauts experiencing weightlessness),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hl_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-hl-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> loss of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body_mass" title="Body mass">body mass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nasal_congestion" title="Nasal congestion">nasal congestion</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sleep_disturbance" title="Sleep disturbance">sleep disturbance</a>, and excess <a href="/wiki/Flatulence" title="Flatulence">flatulence</a>. \n</p><p>The engineering problems associated with leaving <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and developing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_propulsion" title="Space propulsion">space propulsion</a> systems have been examined for over a century, and millions of man-hours of research have been spent on them. In recent years there has been an increase in research on the issue of how humans can survive and work in space for extended and possibly indefinite periods of time. This question requires input from the physical and biological sciences and has now become the greatest challenge (other than funding) facing human <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">space exploration</a>. A fundamental step in overcoming this challenge is trying to understand the effects and impact of long-term space travel on the human body.\n</p><p>In October 2015, the <a href="/wiki/NASA_Office_of_Inspector_General" title="NASA Office of Inspector General">NASA Office of Inspector General</a> issued a health hazards report related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_space_exploration" title="Human space exploration">space exploration</a>, including a <a href="/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars" title="Human mission to Mars">human mission</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151029_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151029-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20151029oig_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20151029oig-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Physiological_effects"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Physiological effects</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Research"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Research</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 deterioration</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-11"><a href="#Fluid_redistribution"><span class="tocnumber">1.4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Fluid redistribution</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-12"><a href="#Disruption_of_senses"><span class="tocnumber">1.4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Disruption of senses</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-4 class="tocnumber">1.4.4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Taste</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-15"><a href="#Additional_physiological_effects"><span class="tocnumber">1.4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Additional physiological effects</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a tocsection-20"><a href="#Duration_of_space_travel"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Duration of space travel</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiological_effects">Physiological effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Physiological effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Many of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environmental conditions</a> experienced by humans during <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">spaceflight</a> are very different from those in which humans evolved; however, technology such as that offered by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spaceship" title="Spaceship">spaceship</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacesuit" title="Spacesuit">spacesuit</a> is able to shield people from the harshest conditions. The immediate needs for breathable air and drinkable water are addressed by a <a href="/wiki/Life_support_system" title="Life support system">life support system</a>, a group of devices that allow human beings to survive in outer space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The life support system supplies <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">air</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> and <a href="/wiki/Space_food" title="Space food">food</a>. It must also maintain temperature and pressure within acceptable limits and <a href="/wiki/Space_toilet" title="Space toilet">deal with the body\'s waste products</a>. Shielding against harmful external influences such as radiation and micro-meteorites is also necessary.\n</p><p>Some hazards are difficult to mitigate, such as weightlessness, also defined as a <a href="/wiki/Micro-g_environment" title="Micro-g environment">microgravity environment</a>. Living in this type of environment impacts the body in three important ways: loss of <a href="/wiki/Proprioception" title="Proprioception">proprioception</a>, changes in fluid distribution, and deterioration of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musculoskeletal_system" title="Musculoskeletal system">musculoskeletal system</a>.\n</p><p>On 2 November 2017, scientists reported that significant changes in the position and structure of the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> have been found in <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">astronauts</a> who have taken <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">trips in space</a>, based on <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">MRI studies</a>. Astronauts who took longer space trips were associated with greater brain changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM-20171102_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM-20171102-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-QTZ-20171103_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-QTZ-20171103-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2018, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>-funded researchers found that lengthy journeys into <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a>, including travel to the <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">planet Mars</a>, may substantially damage the  <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal tissues</a> of astronauts. The studies support earlier work that found such journeys could significantly damage the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brains</a> of <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">astronauts</a>, and age them prematurely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TI-20181002_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-TI-20181002-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_medicine" title="Space medicine">Space medicine</a></div>\n<p>Space medicine is a developing <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medical practice</a> that studies the <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">health</a> of astronauts living in outer space. The main purpose of this academic pursuit is to discover how well and for how long people can survive the extreme conditions in space, and how fast they can re-adapt to the Earth\'s environment after returning from space. Space medicine also seeks to develop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preventive_medicine" title="Preventive medicine">preventative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palliative_care" title="Palliative care">palliative</a> measures to ease the suffering caused by living in an environment to which humans are not well adapted.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ascent_and_reentry">Ascent and reentry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ascent and reentry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/High-G_training" title="High-G training">High-G training</a></div>\n<p>During takeoff and reentry space travelers can experience several times normal gravity. An untrained person can usually withstand about 3g, but can blackout at 4 to 6g. <a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force">G-force</a> in the vertical direction is more difficult to tolerate than a force perpendicular to the spine because blood flows away from the brain and eyes. First the person experiences temporary loss of vision and then at higher g-forces loses consciousness. G-force training and a <a href="/wiki/G-suit" title="G-suit">G-suit</a> which constricts the body to keep more blood in the head can mitigate the effects. Most spacecraft are designed to keep g-forces within comfortable limits.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_environments">Space environments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Space environments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The environment of space is lethal without appropriate protection: the greatest threat in the vacuum of space derives from the lack of oxygen and pressure, although temperature and radiation also pose risks. The effects of space exposure can result in <a href="/wiki/Ebullism" title="Ebullism">ebullism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">hypoxia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypocapnia" title="Hypocapnia">hypocapnia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Decompression_sickness" title="Decompression sickness">decompression sickness</a>. In addition to these, there is also <a href="/wiki/Health_threat_from_cosmic_rays" title="Health threat from cosmic rays">cellular mutation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cell_death" title="Cell death">destruction</a> from high energy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photons" title="Photons">photons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">sub-atomic particles</a> that are present in the surroundings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pilmanis2003_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pilmanis2003-12">[12]</a></sup> Decompression is a serious concern during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity" title="Extra-vehicular activity">extra-vehicular activities</a> (EVAs) of astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conkin2001_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conkin2001-13">[13]</a></sup> Current EMU designs take this and other issues into consideration, and have evolved over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan2005_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan2005-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan2006_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan2006-15">[15]</a></sup> A key challenge has been the competing interests of increasing astronaut mobility (which is reduced by high-pressure <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_Mobility_Unit" title="Extravehicular Mobility Unit">EMUs</a>, analogous to the difficulty of deforming an inflated balloon relative to a deflated one) and minimising <a href="/wiki/Uncontrolled_decompression" title="Uncontrolled decompression">decompression risk</a>. Investigators<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gorguinpour2001_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gorguinpour2001-16">[16]</a></sup> have considered pressurizing a separate head unit to the regular 71 kPa (10.3 psi) cabin pressure as opposed to the current whole-EMU pressure of 29.6&#160;kPa (4.3&#160;psi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan2006_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan2006-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In such a design, pressurization of the torso could be achieved mechanically, avoiding mobility reduction associated with pneumatic pressurization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gorguinpour2001_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gorguinpour2001-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vacuum">Vacuum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Vacuum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Uncontrolled_decompression" title="Uncontrolled decompression">Uncontrolled decompression</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby,_1768.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4160" data-file-width="5639" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby%2C_1768.jpg/220px-An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby%2C_1768.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby%2C_1768.jpg/330px-An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby%2C_1768.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby%2C_1768.jpg/440px-An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby%2C_1768.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_an_Air_Pump_by_Joseph_Wright_of_Derby,_1768.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This painting, <i><a href="/wiki/An_Experiment_on_a_Bird_in_the_Air_Pump" title="An Experiment on a Bird in the Air Pump">An Experiment on a Bird in the Air Pump</a></i> depicts an experiment performed by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> in 1660 to test the effect of a vacuum on a living system.</div></div></div>\n<p>Human physiology is adapted to living within the atmosphere of Earth, and a certain amount of oxygen is required in <a href="/wiki/Breathing_gas" title="Breathing gas">the air we breathe</a>. If the body does not get enough oxygen, then the astronaut is at risk of becoming unconscious and dying from <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">hypoxia</a>. In the vacuum of space, <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">gas exchange</a> in the lungs continues as normal but results in the removal of all gases, including oxygen, from the bloodstream. After 9 to 12 seconds, the deoxygenated blood reaches the brain, and it results in the loss of consciousness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landis_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landis-18">[18]</a></sup> Exposure to vacuum for up to 30 seconds is unlikely to cause permanent physical damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Animal experiments show that rapid and complete recovery is normal for exposures shorter than 90 seconds, while longer full-body exposures are fatal and resuscitation has never been successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greene_Body_In_A_Vacuum_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greene_Body_In_A_Vacuum-21">[21]</a></sup> There is only a limited amount of data available from human accidents, but it is consistent with animal data. Limbs may be exposed for much longer if breathing is not impaired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harding_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-harding-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 1966, <a href="/wiki/Aerospace_engineering" title="Aerospace engineering">aerospace engineer</a> and test subject Jim LeBlanc of <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> was partaking in a test to see how well a pressurized <a href="/wiki/Apollo/Skylab_A7L" title="Apollo/Skylab A7L">space suit prototype</a> would perform in vacuum conditions. To simulate the effects of space, NASA constructed a massive <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_chamber" title="Vacuum chamber">vacuum chamber</a> from which all air could be pumped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gizmodo_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-gizmodo-23">[23]</a></sup> At some point during the test, LeBlanc\'s pressurization hose became detached from the space suit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-awesci_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-awesci-24">[24]</a></sup> Even though this caused his suit pressure to drop from 3.8&#160;psi (26.2&#160;kPa) to 0.1&#160;psi (0.7&#160;kPa) in less than 10 seconds, LeBlanc remained conscious for about 14 seconds before <a href="/wiki/Unconsciousness" title="Unconsciousness">losing consciousness</a> due to hypoxia; the much lower pressure outside the body causes rapid de-oxygenation of the blood. &#8220;As I stumbled backwards, I could feel the saliva on my tongue starting to bubble just before I went unconscious and that&#8217;s the last thing I remember,&#8221; recalls LeBlanc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space_safety_magazine_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-space_safety_magazine-25">[25]</a></sup> The chamber was rapidly pressurized and LeBlanc was given emergency oxygen 25 seconds later. He recovered almost immediately with just an earache and no permanent damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popular_mechanics_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-popular_mechanics-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vision_times_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-vision_times-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another effect from a vacuum is a condition is called <a href="/wiki/Ebullism" title="Ebullism">ebullism</a> which results from the formation of bubbles in body fluids due to reduced ambient pressure, the steam may bloat the body to twice its normal size and slow circulation, but tissues are elastic and porous enough to prevent rupture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Bio_Data_Book_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Bio_Data_Book-28">[28]</a></sup>  Technically, ebullism is considered to begin at an elevation of around 19 kilometres (12&#160;mi) or pressures less than 6.3 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">kPa</a> (47 <a href="/wiki/Torr" title="Torr">mm Hg</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Bio_Data_Book2_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Bio_Data_Book2-29">[29]</a></sup> known as the <a href="/wiki/Armstrong_limit" title="Armstrong limit">Armstrong limit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pilmanis2003_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pilmanis2003-12">[12]</a></sup> Experiments with other animals have revealed an array of symptoms that could also apply to humans. The least severe of these is the freezing of bodily secretions due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evaporative_cooling" title="Evaporative cooling">evaporative cooling</a>. Severe symptoms, such as <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">loss of oxygen in tissue</a>, followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circulatory_failure" title="Circulatory failure">circulatory failure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flaccid_paralysis" title="Flaccid paralysis">flaccid paralysis</a> would occur in about 30 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pilmanis2003_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pilmanis2003-12">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Atelectasis" title="Atelectasis">lungs also collapse</a> in this process, but will continue to release water vapour leading to cooling and ice formation in the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">respiratory tract</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pilmanis2003_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pilmanis2003-12">[12]</a></sup> A rough estimate is that a human will have about 90 seconds to be recompressed, after which death may be unavoidable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Bio_Data_Book_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Bio_Data_Book-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landis_Vacuum_Exposure_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landis_Vacuum_Exposure-30">[30]</a></sup> Swelling from ebullism can be reduced by containment in a <a href="/wiki/Flight_suit" title="Flight suit">flight suit</a> which are necessary to prevent ebullism above 19&#160;km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harding_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-harding-22">[22]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program" title="Space Shuttle program">Space Shuttle program</a> astronauts wore a fitted elastic garment called a Crew Altitude Protection Suit (CAPS) which prevented ebullism at pressures as low as 2 kPa (15 Torr).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The only humans known to have died of space exposure are the three crew members of the <i><a href="/wiki/Soyuz_11" title="Soyuz 11">Soyuz 11</a></i> spacecraft: <a href="/wiki/Vladislav_Volkov" title="Vladislav Volkov">Vladislav Volkov</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgi_Dobrovolski" title="Georgi Dobrovolski">Georgi Dobrovolski</a> and <a href="/wiki/Viktor_Patsayev" title="Viktor Patsayev">Viktor Patsayev</a>. During re-entry on June 30, 1971, the ship\'s depressurization resulted in the death of the entire crew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stewart2007_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart2007-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_July_12,_1971_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Time_July_12,_1971-33">[33]</a></sup> Two other people were decompressed accidentally during space mission training programs on the ground, but both incidents were less than 5 minutes in duration, and both victims survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pilmanis2003_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-Pilmanis2003-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature">Temperature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Temperature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In a vacuum, there is no medium for removing heat from the body by conduction or convection. Loss of heat is by radiation from the 310 K temperature of a person to the 3 K of outer space. This is a slow process, especially in a clothed person, so there is no danger of immediately freezing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Rapid evaporative cooling of skin moisture in a vacuum may create frost, particularly in the mouth, but this is not a significant hazard.\n</p><p>Exposure to the intense <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> of direct, unfiltered <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> would lead to local heating, though that would likely be well distributed by the body\'s conductivity and blood circulation. Other solar radiation, particularly <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> rays, however, may cause severe sunburn.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation">Radiation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Radiation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_threat_from_cosmic_rays" title="Health threat from cosmic rays">Health threat from cosmic rays</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA17601-Comparisons-RadiationExposure-MarsTrip-20131209.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1017" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/PIA17601-Comparisons-RadiationExposure-MarsTrip-20131209.png/250px-PIA17601-Comparisons-RadiationExposure-MarsTrip-20131209.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/PIA17601-Comparisons-RadiationExposure-MarsTrip-20131209.png/375px-PIA17601-Comparisons-RadiationExposure-MarsTrip-20131209.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/PIA17601-Comparisons-RadiationExposure-MarsTrip-20131209.png/500px-PIA17601-Comparisons-RadiationExposure-MarsTrip-20131209.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA17601-Comparisons-RadiationExposure-MarsTrip-20131209.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of Radiation Doses &#8211; includes the amount detected on the trip from Earth to Mars by the <a href="/wiki/Radiation_assessment_detector" title="Radiation assessment detector">RAD</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Mars Science Laboratory">MSL</a> (2011&#8211;2013).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20130531a_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20130531a-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20130531b_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20130531b-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20130530_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20130530-37">[37]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Without the protection of Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> astronauts are exposed to high levels of <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a>. A year in <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> results in a dose of radiation 10 times that of the annual dose on earth.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> High levels of radiation damage <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lymphocytes" title="Lymphocytes">lymphocytes</a>, cells heavily involved in maintaining the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>; this damage contributes to the lowered <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a> experienced by astronauts. Radiation has also recently been linked to a higher incidence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cataracts" title="Cataracts">cataracts</a> in astronauts. Outside the protection of low Earth orbit, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_cosmic_ray" title="Galactic cosmic ray">galactic cosmic rays</a> present further challenges to human spaceflight,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> as the <a href="/wiki/Health_threat_from_cosmic_rays" title="Health threat from cosmic rays">health threat from cosmic rays</a> significantly increases the chances of cancer over a decade or more of exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>-supported study reported that radiation may harm the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronauts" title="Astronauts">astronauts</a> and accelerate the onset of <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer\'s disease">Alzheimer\'s disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLOS-20121231_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-PLOS-20121231-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpaceRef-20130101_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpaceRef-20130101-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NasaWatch-20130103_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-NasaWatch-20130103-43">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">Solar flare</a> events (though rare) can give a fatal radiation dose in minutes. It is thought that protective shielding and protective drugs may ultimately lower the risks to an acceptable level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JCB_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-JCB-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Crew living on the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS) are partially protected from the space environment by Earth\'s magnetic field, as the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> deflects <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> around the earth and the ISS. Nevertheless, solar flares are powerful enough to warp and penetrate the magnetic defences, and so are still a hazard to the crew. The crew of <a href="/wiki/Expedition_10" title="Expedition 10">Expedition 10</a> took shelter as a precaution in 2005 in a more heavily shielded part of the station designed for this purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> However, beyond the limited protection of Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>, interplanetary manned missions are much more vulnerable. Lawrence Townsend of the University of Tennessee and others have studied <a href="/wiki/Solar_storm_of_1859" title="Solar storm of 1859">the most powerful solar flare ever recorded</a>. Radiation doses astronauts would receive from a flare of this magnitude could cause acute radiation sickness and possibly even death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:300px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:640px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Aurora_Australis.ogv/640px--Aurora_Australis.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:640px;height:426px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="33.222222222222" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Aurora_Australis.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/Aurora_Australis.ogv" type="video/ogg;
