codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="362" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="247656" data-framerate="9"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/f0/Aurora_Australis.ogv/Aurora_Australis.ogv.360p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="542" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="167096" data-framerate="9"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/f0/Aurora_Australis.ogv/Aurora_Australis.ogv.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="542" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="500296" data-framerate="9"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'><img alt="File:Aurora Australis.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Aurora_Australis.ogv/300px--Aurora_Australis.ogv.jpg" style="width:300px;height:199px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/Aurora_Australis.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aurora_Australis.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A video made by the crew of the International Space Station showing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_Australis" title="Aurora Australis">Aurora Australis</a>, which is caused by high-energy particles in the space environment.</div></div></div>\n<p>There is scientific concern that extended spaceflight might slow down the body\'s ability to protect itself against diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Radiation can penetrate living tissue and cause both short and long-term damage to the bone marrow stem cells which create the blood and immune systems. In particular, it causes \'<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromosomal" title="Chromosomal">chromosomal</a> aberrations\' in <a href="/wiki/Lymphocyte" title="Lymphocyte">lymphocytes</a>. As these cells are central to the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>, any damage weakens the immune system, which means that in addition to increased vulnerability to new exposures, <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> already present in the body&#8212;which would normally be suppressed&#8212;become active. In space, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-cells" title="T-cells">T-cells</a> (a form of lymphocyte) are less able to reproduce properly, and the T-cells that do reproduce are less able to fight off infection. Over time immunodeficiency results in the rapid spread of infection among crew members, especially in the confined areas of space flight systems.\n</p><p>On 31 May 2013, The NASA scientists reported that a possible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manned_mission_to_Mars" title="Manned mission to Mars">manned mission to Mars</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WIRED-20140212_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-WIRED-20140212-49">[49]</a></sup> may involve a great <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation risk</a> based on the amount of <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">energetic particle radiation</a> detected by the <a href="/wiki/Radiation_assessment_detector" title="Radiation assessment detector">RAD</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Mars Science Laboratory">Mars Science Laboratory</a> while traveling from the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> in 2011&#8211;2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20130531a_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20130531a-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20130531b_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20130531b-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20130530_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20130530-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2017, NASA reported <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation levels</a> on the surface of the planet <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> were temporarily <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(radiation)" title="Orders of magnitude (radiation)">doubled</a>, and were associated with an <a href="/wiki/Aurora" title="Aurora">aurora</a> 25-times brighter than any observed earlier, due to a massive, and unexpected, <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">solar storm</a> in the middle of the month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PHYS-20170930_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-PHYS-20170930-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weightlessness">Weightlessness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Weightlessness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Foale_ZeroG.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2008" data-file-width="3032" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Foale_ZeroG.jpg/220px-Foale_ZeroG.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Foale_ZeroG.jpg/330px-Foale_ZeroG.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Foale_ZeroG.jpg/440px-Foale_ZeroG.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Foale_ZeroG.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Astronauts on the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">ISS</a> in weightless conditions. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foale" title="Michael Foale">Michael Foale</a> can be seen exercising in the foreground.</div></div></div>\n<p>Following the advent of <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space stations</a> that can be inhabited for long periods of time, exposure to <a href="/wiki/Weightlessness" title="Weightlessness">weightlessness</a> has been demonstrated to have some deleterious effects on human health. Humans are well-adapted to the physical conditions at the surface of the earth, and so in response to weightlessness, various <a href="/wiki/Biological_system" title="Biological system">physiological systems</a> begin to change, and in some cases, <a href="/wiki/Atrophy" title="Atrophy">atrophy</a>. Though these changes are usually temporary, some do have a long-term impact on human <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">health</a>.\n</p><p>Short-term exposure to microgravity causes <a href="/wiki/Space_adaptation_syndrome" title="Space adaptation syndrome">space adaptation syndrome</a>, a self-limiting nausea caused by derangement of the <a href="/wiki/Vestibular_system" title="Vestibular system">vestibular system</a>. Long-term exposure causes multiple health problems, one of the most significant being loss of bone and muscle mass. Over time these <a href="/wiki/Deconditioning" title="Deconditioning">deconditioning</a> effects can impair astronauts\' performance, increase their risk of injury, reduce their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerobic_capacity" title="Aerobic capacity">aerobic capacity</a>, and slow down their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_system" title="Cardiovascular system">cardiovascular system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> As the human body consists mostly of fluids, gravity tends to force them into the lower half of the body, and our bodies have many systems to balance this situation. When released from the pull of gravity, these systems continue to work, causing a general redistribution of fluids into the upper half of the body. This is the cause of the round-faced \'puffiness\' seen in astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JCB_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-JCB-44">[44]</a></sup> Redistributing fluids around the body itself causes balance disorders, <a href="/wiki/Distorted_vision" title="Distorted vision">distorted vision</a>, and a loss of taste and smell.\n</p><p>A 2006 Space Shuttle experiment found that <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salmonella_typhimurium" title="Salmonella typhimurium">Salmonella typhimurium</a></i>, a bacterium that can cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_poisoning" title="Food poisoning">food poisoning</a>, became more virulent when cultivated in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASU-20070923_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASU-20070923-52">[52]</a></sup> On April 29, 2013, scientists in Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute, funded by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>, reported that, during <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">spaceflight</a> on the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbes" title="Microbes">microbes</a> seem to adapt to the <a href="/wiki/Space_environment" title="Space environment">space environment</a> in ways "not observed on Earth" and in ways that "can lead to increases in growth and <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulence</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLos-20130429_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-PLos-20130429-53">[53]</a></sup> More recently, in 2017, <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> were found to be more resistant to <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> and to thrive in the near-weightlessness of space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GZM-20170913_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-GZM-20170913-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">Microorganisms</a> have been observed to survive the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> of outer space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dose_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dose-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horneck_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horneck-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Motion_sickness">Motion sickness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Motion sickness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Space_adaptation_syndrome" title="Space adaptation syndrome">Space adaptation syndrome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Motion_sickness" title="Motion sickness">Motion sickness</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg/220px-Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg/330px-Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg/440px-Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bruce_McCandless_II_during_EVA_in_1984.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bruce McCandless floating free in orbit with a <a href="/wiki/Space_suit" title="Space suit">space suit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manned_Maneuvering_Unit" title="Manned Maneuvering Unit">Manned Maneuvering Unit</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The most common problem experienced by humans in the initial hours of weightlessness is known as <a href="/wiki/Space_adaptation_syndrome" title="Space adaptation syndrome">space adaptation syndrome</a> or SAS, commonly referred to as space sickness. It is related to <a href="/wiki/Motion_sickness" title="Motion sickness">motion sickness</a>, and arises as the <a href="/wiki/Vestibular_system" title="Vestibular system">vestibular system</a> adapts to weightlessness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedaily_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedaily-57">[57]</a></sup> Symptoms of SAS include <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertigo_(medical)" title="Vertigo (medical)">vertigo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headaches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lethargy" title="Lethargy">lethargy</a>, and overall malaise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spp22_15_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-spp22_15-2">[2]</a></sup> The first case of SAS was reported by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmonaut" title="Cosmonaut">cosmonaut</a> <a href="/wiki/Gherman_Titov" title="Gherman Titov">Gherman Titov</a> in 1961. Since then, roughly 45% of all people who have flown in space have suffered from this condition. The duration of space sickness varies, but rarely has it lasted for more than 72 hours, after which the body adjusts to the new environment.\n</p><p>On Earth, our bodies react automatically to gravity, maintaining both posture and locomotion in a downward pulling world. In microgravity environments, these constant signals are absent: the <a href="/wiki/Otolith" title="Otolith">otolith</a> organs in the inner <a href="/wiki/Ear" title="Ear">ear</a> are sensitive to linear acceleration and no longer perceive a downwards bias; muscles are no longer required to contract to maintain posture, and pressure receptors in the feet and ankles no longer signal the direction of "down". These changes can immediately result in visual-orientation illusions where the astronaut feels he has flipped 180 degrees.  Over half of astronauts also experience symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Motion_sickness" title="Motion sickness">motion sickness</a> for the first three days of travel due to the conflict between what the body expects and what the body actually perceives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Over time however the brain adapts and although these illusions can still occur, most astronauts begin to see "down" as where the feet are. People returning to Earth after extended weightless periods have to readjust to the force of gravity and may have problems standing up, focusing their gaze, walking and turning. This is just an initial problem, as they recover these abilities quickly.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (June 2012)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> jokingly measures SAS using the "Garn scale", named for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Senator" title="United States Senator">United States Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/Jake_Garn" title="Jake Garn">Jake Garn</a>, whose sickness during <a href="/wiki/STS-51-D" title="STS-51-D">STS-51-D</a> was the worst on record. Accordingly, one "Garn" is equivalent to the most severe possible case of space sickness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> By studying how changes can affect balance in the human body&#8212;involving the senses, the brain, the inner ear, and blood pressure&#8212;NASA hopes to develop treatments that can be used on Earth and in space to correct balance disorders. Until then, astronauts rely on medication, such as <a href="/wiki/Midodrine" title="Midodrine">midodrine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dimenhydrinate" title="Dimenhydrinate">dimenhydrinate</a> anti-nausea patches, as required (such as when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_suits" title="Space suits">space suits</a> are worn, because vomiting into a space suit could be fatal).\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bone_and_muscle_deterioration">Bone and muscle deterioration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Bone and muscle deterioration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight_osteopenia" title="Spaceflight osteopenia">Spaceflight osteopenia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2261" data-file-width="2864" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg/220px-Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg/330px-Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg/440px-Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aboard the International Space Station, astronaut <a href="/wiki/Frank_De_Winne" title="Frank De Winne">Frank De Winne</a> is attached to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COLBERT" title="COLBERT">COLBERT</a> with bungee cords</div></div></div>\n<p>A major effect of long-term weightlessness involves the loss of <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a> mass. Without the effects of gravity, <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle" title="Skeletal muscle">skeletal muscle</a> is no longer required to maintain posture and the muscle groups used in moving around in a weightless environment differ from those required in terrestrial locomotion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In a weightless environment, astronauts put almost no weight on the back <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a> or leg muscles used for standing up.  Those muscles then start to weaken and eventually get smaller. Consequently, some muscles atrophy rapidly, and without regular exercise astronauts can lose up to 20% of their muscle mass in just 5 to 11 days<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle_fibre" title="Muscle fibre">muscle fibre</a> prominent in muscles also change. Slow twitch endurance fibres used to maintain posture are replaced by fast twitch rapidly contracting fibres that are insufficient for any heavy labour. Advances in research on exercise, hormone supplements and medication may help maintain muscle and body mass.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">Bone</a> metabolism also changes. Normally, bone is laid down in the direction of mechanical stress. However, in a microgravity environment there is very little mechanical stress. This results in a <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight_osteopenia" title="Spaceflight osteopenia">loss of bone tissue</a> approximately 1.5% per month especially from the lower vertebrae, hip and femur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAbones_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAbones-61">[61]</a></sup> Due to microgravity and the decreased load on the bones, there is a rapid increase in bone loss, from 3% cortical bone loss per decade to about 1% every month the body is exposed to microgravity, for an otherwise healthy adult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The rapid change in bone density is dramatic, making bones frail and resulting in symptoms which resemble those of osteoporosis. On Earth, the bones are constantly being shed and regenerated through a well-balanced system which involves signaling of osteoblasts and osteoclasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> These systems are coupled, so that whenever bone is broken down, newly formed layers take its place&#8212;neither should happen without the other, in a healthy adult. In space, however, there is an increase in osteoclast activity due to microgravity. This is a problem, because osteoclasts break down the bones into minerals that are reabsorbed by the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Osteoblasts are not consecutively active with the osteoclasts, causing the bone to be constantly diminished with no recovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> This increase in osteoclasts activity has been seen particularly in the pelvic region, because this is the region which carries the biggest load with gravity present. A study demonstrated that in healthy mice, osteoclasts appearance increased by 197%, accompanied by a down-regulation of osteoblasts and growth factors that are known to help with the formation of new bone, after only sixteen days of exposure to microgravity. Elevated blood <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> levels from the lost bone result in dangerous calcification of soft tissues and potential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kidney_stone" title="Kidney stone">kidney stone</a> formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAbones_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASAbones-61">[61]</a></sup> It is still unknown whether bone recovers completely. Unlike people with osteoporosis, astronauts eventually regain their bone density.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> After a 3&#8211;4 month trip into space, it takes about 2&#8211;3 years to regain lost bone density.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> New techniques are being developed to help astronauts recover faster. Research on diet, exercise and medication may hold the potential to aid the process of growing new bone.\n</p><p>To prevent some of these adverse <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiological" title="Physiological">physiological</a> effects, the ISS is equipped with two treadmills (including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treadmill_with_Vibration_Isolation_System" title="Treadmill with Vibration Isolation System">COLBERT</a>), and the aRED (advanced Resistive Exercise Device), which enable various weight-lifting exercises which add muscle but do nothing for bone density,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> and a stationary bicycle; each astronaut spends at least two hours per day exercising on the equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESALife_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESALife-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASACrewEquip_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASACrewEquip-68">[68]</a></sup> Astronauts use bungee cords to strap themselves to the treadmill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Astronauts subject to long periods of weightlessness wear pants with elastic bands attached between waistband and cuffs to compress the leg bones and reduce osteopenia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hl_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-hl-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Currently, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> is using advanced computational tools to understand how to best counteract the  bone and muscle atrophy experienced by astronauts in microgravity environments for prolonged periods of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>   The Human Research Program\'s Human Health Countermeasures Element chartered the Digital Astronaut Project to investigate targeted questions about exercise countermeasure regimes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>   NASA is focusing on integrating a model of the advanced Resistive Exercise Device (ARED) currently on board the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> with <a href="/wiki/OpenSim_(simulation_toolkit)" title="OpenSim (simulation toolkit)">OpenSim</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> musculoskeletal models of humans exercising with the device.  The goal of this work is to use inverse dynamics to estimate joint torques and muscle forces resulting from using the ARED, and thus more accurately prescribe exercise regimens for the astronauts.  These joint torques and muscle forces could be used in conjunction with more fundamental computational simulations of bone remodeling and muscle adaptation in order to more completely model the end effects of such countermeasures, and determine whether a proposed exercise regime would be sufficient to sustain astronaut musculoskeletal health.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fluid_redistribution">Fluid redistribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Fluid redistribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:295px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Space_fluid_shift.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="151" data-file-width="293" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/50/Space_fluid_shift.gif" width="293"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Space_fluid_shift.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The effects of microgravity on fluid distribution around the body (greatly exaggerated).</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clayton_Anderson_zero_g.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="780" data-file-width="1322" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Clayton_Anderson_zero_g.jpg/220px-Clayton_Anderson_zero_g.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Clayton_Anderson_zero_g.jpg/330px-Clayton_Anderson_zero_g.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Clayton_Anderson_zero_g.jpg/440px-Clayton_Anderson_zero_g.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clayton_Anderson_zero_g.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Astronaut <a href="/wiki/Clayton_Anderson" title="Clayton Anderson">Clayton Anderson</a> observes as a water bubble floats in front of him on the Discovery. Water <a href="/wiki/Cohesion_(chemistry)" title="Cohesion (chemistry)">cohesion</a> plays a bigger role in microgravity than on Earth</div></div></div>\n<p>The second effect of weightlessness takes place in human fluids. The body is made up of 60% water, much of it intra-vascular and inter-cellular. Within a few moments of entering a microgravity environment, fluid is immediately re-distributed to the upper body resulting in bulging neck veins, puffy face and sinus and nasal congestion which can last throughout the duration of the trip and is very much like the symptoms of the common cold. In space the autonomic reactions of the body to maintain blood pressure are not required and fluid is distributed more widely around the whole body. This results in a decrease in <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">plasma</a> volume of around 20%. These fluid shifts initiate a cascade of adaptive systemic effects that can be dangerous upon return to earth. <a href="/wiki/Orthostatic_intolerance" title="Orthostatic intolerance">Orthostatic intolerance</a> results in astronauts returning to Earth after extended space missions being unable to stand unassisted for more than 10 minutes at a time without fainting. This is due in part to changes in the autonomic regulation of blood pressure and the loss of plasma volume. Although this effect becomes worse the longer the time spent in space, as yet all individuals have returned to normal within at most a few weeks of landing.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>In space, astronauts lose fluid volume&#8212;including up to 22% of their blood volume. Because it has less blood to pump, the heart will <a href="/wiki/Atrophy" title="Atrophy">atrophy</a>. A weakened heart results in low blood pressure and can produce a problem with "orthostatic tolerance", or the body\'s ability to send enough oxygen to the brain without the astronaut\'s fainting or becoming dizzy. "Under the effects of the earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, blood and other body fluids are pulled towards the lower body. When gravity is taken away or reduced during space exploration, the blood tends to collect in the upper body instead, resulting in facial <a href="/wiki/Edema" title="Edema">edema</a> and other unwelcome side effects. Upon return to earth, the blood begins to pool in the lower extremities again, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Orthostatic_hypotension" title="Orthostatic hypotension">orthostatic hypotension</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Disruption_of_senses">Disruption of senses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Disruption of senses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Vision">Vision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Vision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>In 2013 NASA published a study that found changes to the eyes and eyesight of monkeys with spaceflights longer than 6 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aao.org_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-aao.org-76">[76]</a></sup> Noted changes included a flattening of the eyeball and changes to the retina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aao.org_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-aao.org-76">[76]</a></sup> Space traveler\'s eye-sight can become blurry after too much time in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SKR-20171103_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-SKR-20171103-78">[78]</a></sup> Another effect is known as <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray_visual_phenomena" title="Cosmic ray visual phenomena">Cosmic ray visual phenomena</a>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...[a] NASA survey of 300 male and female astronauts, about 23 percent of short-flight and 49 percent of long-flight astronauts said they had experienced problems with both near and distance vision during their missions. Again, for some people vision problems persisted for years afterward.</p><div class="templatequotecite">&#8212;&#8201;<cite>NASA<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aao.org_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-aao.org-76">[76]</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Since dust can not settle in zero gravity, small pieces of dead skin or metal can get in the eye, causing irritation and increasing the risk of infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Intracranial pressure</dt></dl>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Visual_impairment_due_to_intracranial_pressure" title="Visual impairment due to intracranial pressure">Visual impairment due to intracranial pressure</a></div>\n<p>Because weightlessness increases the amount of fluid in the upper part of the body, astronauts experience increased <a href="/wiki/Intracranial_pressure" title="Intracranial pressure">intracranial pressure</a>. This appears to increase pressure on the backs of the eyeballs, affecting their shape and slightly crushing the <a href="/wiki/Optic_nerve" title="Optic nerve">optic nerve</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20140127_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140127-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mader-2011_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mader-2011-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Puiu-20111109_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Puiu-20111109-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN-20120109_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-20120109-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20120313_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20120313-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radiology-20120313_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Radiology-20120313-84">[84]</a></sup>  This effect was noticed in 2012 in a study using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI" title="MRI">MRI</a> scans of astronauts who had returned to Earth following at least one month in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Such eyesight problems could be a major concern for future deep space flight missions, including a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manned_mission" title="Manned mission">manned mission</a> to the planet <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WIRED-20140212_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-WIRED-20140212-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mader-2011_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mader-2011-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Puiu-20111109_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Puiu-20111109-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN-20120109_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-20120109-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20120313_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20120313-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>If indeed elevated intracranial pressure is the cause, artificial gravity might present one solution, as it would for many human health risks in space. However, such artificial gravitational systems have yet to be proven. More, even with sophisticated artificial gravity, a state of relative microgravity may remain, the risks of which remain unknown.\n<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Taste">Taste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Taste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>One effect of weightlessness on humans is that some astronauts report a change in their sense of <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">taste</a> when in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Some astronauts find that their food is bland, others find that their favorite foods no longer taste as good (one who enjoyed coffee disliked the taste so much on a mission that he stopped drinking it after returning to Earth); some astronauts enjoy eating certain foods that they would not normally eat, and some experience no change whatsoever. Multiple tests have not identified the cause,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bourland20060407_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-bourland20060407-89">[89]</a></sup> and several theories have been suggested, including food degradation, and psychological changes such as boredom. Astronauts often choose strong-tasting food to combat the loss of taste.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Additional_physiological_effects">Additional physiological effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Additional physiological effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>After two months, calluses on the bottoms of feet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molt#Humans" title="Molt">molt</a> and fall off from lack of use, leaving soft new skin. Tops of feet become, by contrast, raw and painfully sensitive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pettit20120504_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-pettit20120504-90">[90]</a></sup> Tears cannot be shed while crying, as they stick together into a ball.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garber20130114_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-garber20130114-91">[91]</a></sup> In microgravity odors quickly permeate the environment, and NASA found in a test that the smell of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cream_sherry" title="Cream sherry">cream sherry</a> triggered the gag reflex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bourland20060407_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-bourland20060407-89">[89]</a></sup> Various other physical discomforts such as back and abdominal pain are common because of the readjustment to gravity, where in space there was no gravity and these muscles could freely stretch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> These may be part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asthenization" title="Asthenization">asthenization</a> syndrome reported by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmonauts" title="Cosmonauts">cosmonauts</a> living in space over an extended period of time, but regarded as anecdotal by astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Fatigue, listlessness, and psychosomatic worries are also part of the syndrome.  The data is inconclusive; however, the syndrome does appear to exist as a manifestation of all the internal and external stress crews in space must face.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychological_effects">Psychological effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Psychological effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Psychological_and_sociological_effects_of_spaceflight" title="Psychological and sociological effects of spaceflight">Psychological and sociological effects of spaceflight</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moonmir_sts91_big.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2020" data-file-width="2151" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Moonmir_sts91_big.jpg/220px-Moonmir_sts91_big.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Moonmir_sts91_big.jpg/330px-Moonmir_sts91_big.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Moonmir_sts91_big.jpg/440px-Moonmir_sts91_big.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moonmir_sts91_big.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Studies of Russian cosmonauts, such as those on <a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a>, provide data on the long-term effects of space on the human body.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_2">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The psychological effects of living in space have not been clearly analyzed but analogies on Earth do exist, such as <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> research stations and <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a>. The enormous stress on the crew, coupled with the body adapting to other environmental changes, can result in anxiety, insomnia and depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dickens-MR-2017-03_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dickens-MR-2017-03-94">[94]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stress">Stress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Stress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>There has been considerable evidence that psychosocial stressors are among the most important impediments to optimal crew morale and performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Cosmonaut <a href="/wiki/Valery_Ryumin" title="Valery Ryumin">Valery Ryumin</a>, twice Hero of the Soviet Union, quotes this passage from <i>The Handbook of Hymen</i> by <a href="/wiki/O._Henry" title="O. Henry">O. Henry</a> in his autobiographical book about the Salyut 6 mission: "If you want to instigate the art of manslaughter just shut two men up in a eighteen by twenty-foot cabin for a month. Human nature won\'t stand it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p>NASA\'s interest in psychological stress caused by space travel, initially studied when their manned missions began, was rekindled when astronauts joined cosmonauts on the Russian space station Mir. Common sources of stress in early American missions included maintaining high performance while under public scrutiny, as well as isolation from peers and family. On the ISS, the latter is still often a cause of stress, such as when NASA Astronaut <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daniel_Tani" title="Daniel Tani">Daniel Tani</a>\'s mother died in a car accident, and when <a href="/wiki/Michael_Fincke" title="Michael Fincke">Michael Fincke</a> was forced to miss the birth of his second child.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sleep">Sleep</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Sleep">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sleep_in_space" title="Sleep in space">Sleep in space</a></div>\n<p>The amount and quality of <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">sleep</a> experienced in space is poor due to highly variable light and dark cycles on flight decks and poor illumination during daytime hours in the space craft. Even the habit of looking out of the window before retiring can send the wrong messages to the brain, resulting in poor sleep patterns. These disturbances in <a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm" title="Circadian rhythm">circadian rhythm</a> have profound effects on the neurobehavioural responses of crew and aggravate the psychological stresses they already experience (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatigue_and_sleep_loss_during_spaceflight" title="Fatigue and sleep loss during spaceflight">Fatigue and sleep loss during spaceflight</a> for more information). Sleep is disturbed on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISS" title="ISS">ISS</a> regularly due to mission demands, such as the scheduling of incoming or departing space vehicles. Sound levels in the station are unavoidably high because the atmosphere is unable to <a href="/wiki/Thermosiphon" title="Thermosiphon">thermosiphon</a>; fans are required at all times to allow processing of the atmosphere, which would stagnate in the freefall (zero-g) environment. Fifty percent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_shuttle" title="Space shuttle">space shuttle</a> astronauts take sleeping pills and still get 2 hours less sleep each night in space than they do on the ground. NASA is researching two areas which may provide the keys to a better night&#8217;s sleep, as improved sleep decreases fatigue and increases daytime productivity.  A variety of methods for combating this phenomenon are constantly under discussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Duration_of_space_travel">Duration of space travel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Duration of space travel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A study of the longest spaceflight concluded that the first three weeks represent a critical period where attention is adversely affected because of the demand to adjust to the extreme change of environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> While Skylab\'s three crews remained in space 1, 2, and 3 months respectively, long-term crews on Salyut 6, Salyut 7, and the ISS remain about 5&#8211;6 months, while MIR expeditions often lasted longer. The ISS working environment includes further stress caused by living and working in cramped conditions with people from very different cultures who speak different languages. First generation space stations had crews who spoke a single language, while 2nd and 3rd generation stations have a crew from many cultures who speak many languages. The ISS is unique because visitors are not classed automatically into \'host\' or \'guest\' categories as with previous stations and spacecraft, and may not suffer from feelings of isolation in the same way.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_use">Future use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Future use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stanford_torus_under_construction.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1404" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Stanford_torus_under_construction.jpg/220px-Stanford_torus_under_construction.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Stanford_torus_under_construction.jpg/330px-Stanford_torus_under_construction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Stanford_torus_under_construction.jpg/440px-Stanford_torus_under_construction.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stanford_torus_under_construction.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Space colonization efforts must take into account the effects of space on the human body.</div></div></div>\n<p>The sum of human experience has resulted in the accumulation of 58 solar years in space and a much better understanding of how the human body adapts. In the future, <a href="/wiki/Space_manufacturing" title="Space manufacturing">industrialisation of space</a> and exploration of inner and outer planets will require humans to endure longer and longer periods in space. The majority of current data comes from missions of short duration and so some of the long-term physiological effects of living in space are still unknown. A round trip to <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WIRED-20140212_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-WIRED-20140212-49">[49]</a></sup>  with current technology is estimated to involve at least 18 months in transit alone. Knowing how the human body reacts to such time periods in space is a vital part of the preparation for such journeys. On-board medical facilities need to be adequate for coping with any type of trauma or emergency as well as contain a huge variety of diagnostic and medical instruments in order to keep a crew healthy over a long period of time, as these will be the only facilities available on board a spacecraft for coping not only with trauma, but also with the adaptive responses of the human body in space.\n</p><p>At the moment only rigorously tested humans have experienced the conditions of space.  If <a href="/wiki/Space_colonization" title="Space colonization">off-world colonization</a> someday begins, many types of people will be exposed to these dangers, and the effects on the very young are completely unknown. On October 29, 1998, John Glenn, one of the original Mercury 7, returned to space at the age of 77. His space flight, which lasted 9 days, provided NASA with important information about the effects of space flight in older people. Factors such as nutritional requirements and physical environments which have so far not been examined will become important. Overall, there is little data on the manifold effects of living in space, and this makes attempts toward mitigating the risks during a lengthy space habitation difficult. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Test_bed" title="Test bed">Test beds</a> such as the ISS are currently being utilized to research some of these risks.\n</p><p>The environment of space is still largely unknown, and there will likely be as-yet-unknown hazards.  Meanwhile, future technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Artificial_gravity" title="Artificial gravity">artificial gravity</a> and more complex bioregenerative <a href="/wiki/Life_support_system" title="Life support system">life support systems</a> may someday be capable of mitigating some risks.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">\n<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatigue_and_sleep_loss_during_spaceflight" title="Fatigue and sleep loss during spaceflight">Fatigue and sleep loss during spaceflight</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_systems_on_space_exploration_missions" title="Food systems on space exploration missions">Food systems on space exploration missions</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Intervertebral_disc_damage_and_spaceflight" title="Intervertebral disc damage and spaceflight">Intervertebral disc damage and spaceflight</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Locomotion_in_space" title="Locomotion in space">Locomotion in space</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mars_Analog_Habitats" title="Mars Analog Habitats">Mars Analog Habitats</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Medical_treatment_during_spaceflight" title="Medical treatment during spaceflight">Medical treatment during spaceflight</a></li>\n<li><a in space</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Space_colonization" title="Space colonization">Space colonization</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Spaceflight_radiation_carcinogenesis" title="Spaceflight radiation missions">Team composition and cohesion in spaceflight missions</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Visual_impairment_due_to_intracranial_pressure" title="Visual impairment due to intracranial pressure">Visual impairment due to intracranial pressure</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
